        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2f265a65a8df47409d1e760ca54f19cc">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '64a348b0-0f1f-45f2-bbd1-dbc8229c0759'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d173573648" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The stable phase transformation during electrochemical progress drives extensive research on vanadium-based polyanions in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), especially Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP). And the electron transfer between V<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox couple in NVP could be generally achieved, owing to the confined crystal variation during battery service. However, the more favorable V<sup>4+/5+</sup> redox couple is still in hard-to-access situation due to the high barrier and further brings about the corresponding inefficiency in energy densities. In this work, the multilevel redox in NVP frame (MLNP) alters reaction pathway to undergo homeostatic solid solution process and breaks the high barrier of V<sup>4+/5+</sup> at high voltage, taking by progressive transition metal (V, Fe, Ti, and Cr) redox couple. The diversified reaction paths across diffusion barriers could be realized by distinctive release/uptake of inactive Na1 site, confirmed by the calculations of density functional theory. Thereby its volume change is merely 1.73% during the multielectron-transfer process (≈2.77 electrons). MLNP cathode could achieve an impressive energy density of 440&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, driving the leading development of MLNP among other NASICON structure SIBs. The integration of multiple redox couples with low strain modulates the reaction pathway effectively and will open a new avenue for fabricating high-performance cathodes in SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202400690-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202400690-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>A potential lithium resource crisis is anticipated with significant price fluctuations, stemming from the shortage and uneven distribution of lithium resources globally, coupled with the increasing demand for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the market.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0001_R_d173573638e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have the potential to be a practical substitution partially for LIBs since they possess comparable physical and chemical characteristics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0002_R_d173573638e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The utilization of SIBs in major power grids and electric vehicles has been implemented to decrease the need for LIBs and address the ongoing lithium supply issue.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0003_R_d173573638e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Those systems give priority to durability (including cycle life and safety), which is primarily determined by the electrode material (the host of Na<sup>+</sup> storage). The polyanion compounds exhibit superior structural stability and thermal stability than transition metal (TM) layered oxides and Prussian blue analogs in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0004_R_d173573638e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Storage Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, their favorable structure accesses to the precedence, taking the continuous investigations in the development of practical SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Among various polyanionic compounds, Na super ionic conductor (NASICON) material unfolds rapid migration of Na<sup>+</sup> through its distinctive 3D diffusion network structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0005_R_d173573638e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Mater. Chem. A lnorg. Chem. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a typical representative, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP), has the characteristics of facile preparation, favorable potential of capacity, and work voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0006_R_d173573638e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Small Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrochemical reaction processes of V<sup>2+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> redox pairs produce operating voltages of 1.6 and 3.4&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, with theoretical capacities up to 176 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. However, its intrinsic low electronic conductivity arising from the high covalency hampers the actual capacity and aggravates voltage polarization, further restricting the cycling stability, especially at high rates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0007_R_d173573638e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, during phase transitions, the de/intercalation behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> induces volume effects on the main structure, thus impacting structural stability and cyclic durability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0008_R_d173573638e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chin. Phys. B">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the major challenge to NASICON compounds like NVP in the application of SIBs is to take advantage of the structural benefits, while addressing the issues including poor electronic conductivity and volume effect during the reversible insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>Up to now, cutting-edge studies on NVP have elevated its potential by activating the third Na<sup>+</sup> as charge carrier, optimizing the intrinsic structure without changing the main framework, through substituting vanadium with other elements, such as Fe, Cr, Mn, Ni, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0009_R_d173573638e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, part of them participated in NVP could enhance ionic diffusivity and electronic conductivity, benefiting from the effect of hybridized orbitals in total density of states (DOS).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0010_R_d173573638e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett. J. Mater. Sci. Technol. Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence the modulation of electron-filled state in the substitution strategy could effectively improve the electronic transport in NVP by introducing diverse elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0011_R_d173573638e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Sci. Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, there has been related research about layers oxides in SIBs rely on manipulating the electron population to regulate reaction path via the synergistic incorporation of TM ions substitution: the enhanced electron hybridization guides the thermodynamically favorable solid solution pathway replacing of two-phase process, evading the induction of phase boundaries, severe local strain, and cracks, thus rewards the excellent cycling stability and rate performance in unstable oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0012_R_d173573638e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Not just phase transformation reaction, the intermediate solid solution also usually involves inferior structure rearrangement, even spontaneously converting to two coexisting phases along with the insurmountable kinetic barriers of phase interfaces. Therefore, the pathway of homeostatic solid solution possesses the facile Na<sup>+</sup> transport and mitigated phase transition barriers, would create a new avenue for rational designing of cathode materials for SIBs and beyond. In turn, the paramount challenge to NASICON compounds mentioned above would be expected to ameliorate by regulating reaction pathway, taking the utilization of multiple redox reactions while considering dynamic characteristics and stable crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0013_R_d173573638e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, this work combines the simulation via density functional theory (DFT), selects dominant TM ions to gain the isostructural derivative of NVP, Na<sub>3.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>V<sub>0.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (with the multilevel redox, abbreviated for MLNP). In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis confirmed that MLNP adopts homeostatic solid solution course and overcomes the high energy barriers of V<sup>4+/5+</sup> with only a volume effect of 1.37%, making a largely differentiated behavior compared to NVP. MLNP owns the reversible de-/intercalation of 2.77 Na<sup>+</sup> in the formula, with a specific discharge capacity of 176 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C. Furthermore, a superior cycling durability with 0.0061% capacity decay per cycle is achieved after 5000 cycles at 5 C. The fast and stable reaction kinetics stems from the release/uptake of inactive Na1 and depressed energy barrier confirmed by DFT computations, further driving the leading development of MLNP in terms of energy density and cyclic reversibility among the other NASICON structural compounds. This work integrates multiple redox couples through skillful modulation of the reaction pathway, effectively overcoming the barrier with sluggish reactivity to fabricate high-performance NASICON cathodes for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202400690-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202400690-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202400690-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202400690-sec-0030-title">2.1 Theoretical Simulation Prediction and Material Design</h3>
            
            <p>To understand the electron-transport influence taken from the introduction of other TM ions on the intrinsic deficiency of NVP (low electronic conductivity), the partial density of state (PDOS) was used to investigate electron distribution in various 3d orbitals of TM ions. The optimized evolvement models were predicted and estimated by doping some TM ions in preserving the structure feature of NVP. From the various substitution environments with NVP depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information, it is seen that most of them introduce obvious impurity bands in the band gap of NVP, derived from the difference compared to the 3d orbital spin configurations of V<sup>3+</sup>. Their band gap was decreased to less than 0.5&nbsp;eV, significantly lower than that of the pure NVP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0014_R_d173573638e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Ceram. Int.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such an improvement is expected to promote the electronic conduction and exert a positive effect on the reaction kinetics, making an effect on breaking the bottleneck of the low electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0015_R_d173573638e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Their multilevel utilization is shown schematically in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0001">1a</a>: these impurity bands will occur at different sites and almost fill the band gap near the Fermi level. According to the main occupation position, Cr, Fe, and Ti tend to play a role in the conduction band and bring a small number of donors to exist in it, whose introduction contributes to accumulating free electrons thus accelerating the electron transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0016_R_d173573638e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further weighing the proper operating condition, the state in Ni, Zn, and Cu substitution is likely uncontrolled with the high potential or incapable redox activity in the common voltage range.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0017_R_d173573638e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the crystal structure model of NVP simultaneously occupied by preferred TM ions (V, Fe, Ti, Cr) and evolved to the right-hand diagram in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0001">1a</a>, along with activating reversible redox reactions and multiple electron transfer. The equal proportion occupation in NVP is still taking a lantern-like open framework, which consists of corner sharing TMO<sub>6</sub> (TM = Fe/V/Cr/Ti) octahedra and PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0018_R_d173573638e684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A novel NVP counterpart, Na<sub>3.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>V<sub>0.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, was designed with multiple active sites and regarded as MLNP.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202400690-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/108047f1-ec75-41f8-ab94-814207571833/adma202400690-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/108047f1-ec75-41f8-ab94-814207571833/adma202400690-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f9121796-876a-4e14-a7e6-8f460f03d065/adma202400690-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/108047f1-ec75-41f8-ab94-814207571833/adma202400690-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202400690-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202400690" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The screening design on the crystal structure of NVP via doping TM ions. b)The obtained Rietveld refinement results of MLNP on XRD patterns. c) Magnetic hysteresis loop of MLNP and NVP. d) EPR spectra of MLNP and NVP. e–i) Morphological information of MLNP: e) SEM image, f,g) TEM images, h) HR-TEM image with inset FFT image (top right) and line profile (bottom right). i) Corresponding EDS mapping results of MLNP.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As usual, the MLNP material was synthesized via a facile sol-gel method, and the synthesis process can be found in the experimental parts for details. The powder XRD of MLNP and NVP is depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information. The two similar diffraction peaks indicate the good crystallinity, which is associated with the typical NASICON structure. The refined phase information is further employed to determine the constitution of MLNP in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0001">1b</a>. The result of Rietveld refinement indicates that the MLNP adopts a typical trigonal (R-3c) structure and confirms the full solubility of the TM ions in the framework. Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information exhibits the crystal cell parameters of the MLNP and confirms the substitute of V site. The occupancies of two independent Na sites site are also derived from the Rietveld refinement (Na1: 6b site with sixfold coordination and Na2: 18e site with eightfold coordination), with obvious enhancement in Na1 site compared to NVP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0019_R_d173573638e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Colloid Interface Sci. J. Solid State Chem.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0001">1c,d</a>, the magnetic test result visually proves that the introduction of multiple dominant TM ions remarkably increases the effective magnetic moment; and the electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) accurately detects unpaired electronic information of MLNP compared with NVP. That is, the overall electron filling of e<sub>g</sub> orbital changes, which is consistent with the above theoretical prediction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0020_R_d173573638e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, this variation in the local structure crystal contributes to stimulating more active sites and optimizing the electrochemical performance hopefully.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0021_R_d173573638e752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The microstructure of MLNP was visually confirmed by the scanning electron microscope (SEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM), manifesting the bulk morphologies of the micron size in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0001">1e,f</a>. The high-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) was used to further identify the fine structure of MLNP in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0001">1g,h</a>. There is a homogeneous carbon layer on the particle, and its average thickness is only 2&nbsp;nm, which is derived from the pyrolysis and carbonization of citric acid and is in favor of buffering volume change and promoting electronic conductivity effectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0022_R_d173573638e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The HRTEM image of MLNP in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0001">1h</a> also manifests that the clear lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.447&nbsp;nm direct to the (104) plane. Moreover, the fast Fourier transform (FFT) image reconfirms the high crystallinity of the as-prepared sample. The distribution of all the elements in MLNP is verified by SEM energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images, confirming the uniform distribution of the elements in particles and in good agreement with the refined powder diffraction results.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202400690-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202400690-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Properties and Diffusion Dynamics</h3>
            
            <p>The MLNP and NVP were examined and assessed on the electrochemical performance in half cells. Their galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves and cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves clearly communicate the potential plateaux and redox reaction with respective capacity delivery in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. Particularly, the reduction peaks (C1, C2, C3) of MLNP are located at 2.18, 3.42, and 4.07&nbsp;V, in coincidence with the plateau in the GCD curves and based on the Ti<sup>3+/4+</sup>, V<sup>3+/4+</sup>, and V<sup>4+/5+</sup> reactions. At the same time, there are more than 2 Na<sup>+</sup> insertions during the discharge progress, especially plus the redox reaction of V<sup>2+/3+</sup>, Fe<sup>2+/3+</sup>, and Cr<sup>+3/+4</sup> ≈1.56, 2.50, and 4.50&nbsp;V, which delivers specific capacity (<i>C</i><sub>s</sub>) of 173.48 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and still remains stable up to 178.30 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in subsequent two cycles. While in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0002">2b</a>, the half cells of NVP at 0.1 C deliver 148.30, 145.83, 144.45 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the initial three cycles, mainly based on the V<sup>2+/3+</sup> and V<sup>3+/4+</sup> at ≈1.60 and 3.40&nbsp;V in the CV curves. In further detail, the multilevel redox reactions of MLNP compared to NVP were confirmed by d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information: there is an absence of V<sup>4+/5+</sup> at the high potential region even in the same voltage range as MLNP without the problem of electrolyte decomposition, demonstrating the limitation of NVP in stimulating and utilizing the high valence state. Moreover, the curves superposition (GCD, CV, d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i>) of the two implies their promising cycling ability. The cycling ability of MLNP and NVP is revealed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0002">2c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a>, Supporting Information. The two show similar tendency of cycling decay at 1&nbsp;C during 300 cycles, while the coulombic efficiency (CE) of NVP fluctuates more than that of MLNP, demonstrating its inadequate stability of electrochemical processes in withstanding a wide voltage test range, particularly at high voltage. Such an effect is amplified in high-current test (5 C): the half cells of NVP only reserve 21.38% capacity retention after 5000 cycles, far below the counterpart of MLNP (69.48%). The figurative representations in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0002">2c</a> insets are their capacity retention per 1000 cycles. And the distinguishable capacity decay is apparent between MLNP and NVP after 5000 cycles. The result of cycling test implies the superiority of MLNP with the stability in the high current.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202400690-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ad6220bc-2cb9-445e-b1f1-ea485ea8720a/adma202400690-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ad6220bc-2cb9-445e-b1f1-ea485ea8720a/adma202400690-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2f1d9b44-7789-4eef-b860-f75a3b087713/adma202400690-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ad6220bc-2cb9-445e-b1f1-ea485ea8720a/adma202400690-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202400690-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202400690" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The initial three GCD profiles at 0.1 C and CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> tested between 1.5 and 4.5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na of a) MLNP half cells; b) NVP half cells. c) Long-term cycling life of MLNP half cells at 5 C and the illustration of the battery model corresponds to the capacity retention per 1000 cycles. d) CV curves of MLNP half cells at scan rates of ≈0.1–1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> at every redox peak. e) The variation trend of <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> along with voltage in MLNP and NVP during the initial two cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, the CV test at different scan rates was applied to identify the Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics of the individual redox pairs in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0002">2d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information. Their calculations involved are based on related equations to determine the Na<sup>+</sup> apparent diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>), confirmed by the linear relationship between peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) against the square root of the scan rates (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0023_R_d173573638e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> of each redox peak are labeled in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0002">2d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a>, Supporting Information in the form of blue columns. According to calculation of CV result, the reduction peak (C3) has the lowest <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> of MLNP, indicating the mainly limited by the reduction step V<sup>4+/5+</sup> during the discharge progress. Even so, the activation of V<sup>4+/5+</sup> does work in MLNP compared to the traditional NVP. And in the same transformation of V<sup>3+/4+</sup> at 3.4&nbsp;V, the diffusion of MLNP is also superior to NVP. This usability of multivalence state V promotes the multielectron transfer with more 2e<sup>−</sup> of MLNP in the wide voltage range of ≈1.5–4.5&nbsp;V, benefitting from the joint effect of all TM ions. At the same time, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability during the electrochemical process was evaluated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). The GITT curves, the variation trend of <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> along with time, and the single titration curves are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information. The differentiated <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values of MLNP and NVP also reveal their respective sluggish behaviors in specific voltage ranges, depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0002">2e</a>. The inert Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion lies on the ending of charge and discharge processes, most likely related to the redox of V<sup>4+/5+</sup>, Cr<sup>3+/4+</sup>, and V<sup>2+/3+</sup>. Even so, the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> of redox couples in the MLNP cathode trends to stable (at the range of ≈10<sup>−12</sup>–10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>) and the migration behavior is highly coincident in the two cycles. Compared with NVP, there is no prominent diffusion “obstacle” in the charging/discharging courses. The <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> ≈3.4&nbsp;V is plummeted in NVP to 10<sup>−15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, whose phase transition makes Na<sup>+</sup> continuously sluggish diffusion at such voltage. Coincidentally, the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> of the electrochemical process based on V<sup>2+/3+</sup> transformation is markedly at a low level of ≈1.7&nbsp;V. Contrary to NVP, MLNP fluctuates at ≈10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is superior to that of NVP. This weak diffusion at plateaux regions renders NVP lost the durable cycling ability, as the fast capacity fading shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0002">2c</a>. Overall, both CV and GITT tests manifest the overall elevation of MLNP in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion behavior, which makes profit in achieving the desired perspective of electrochemical performance.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202400690-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202400690-sec-0050-title">2.3 Na<sup>+</sup> Migration Mechanism in Multilevel Redox Process</h3>
            
            <p>Notably, the multilevel redox reaction accelerates the Na<sup>+</sup> migration and obtains elevated capacity, according to the above actual results. It is very likely related to the differentiated local environments from the various TM elements in MLNP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0010_R_d173573638e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett. J. Mater. Sci. Technol. Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the possible Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathways and barriers of MLNP and NVP were estimated on differentiated local environments by climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method. The corresponding calculation details and atomic coordinates are in the calculation section of Supporting Information and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information. The migration on Na1 site itself often has a considerable energy barrier from its inactivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0010_R_d173573638e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett. J. Mater. Sci. Technol. Adv. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202400690-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0024_R_d173573638e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Energy Lett.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the independent Na sites (Na1 and Na2), two possible pathways (i.e., Na1↔Na2 and Na2 ↔ Na2) were calculated to uncover the Na<sup>+</sup> migration in MLNP and NVP. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information, the energy conversion of Na1↔Na2 migration pathways verifies the fixed propensity of Na1 (the processes of Na1→Na2 are all endothermic with high barriers, while the inverse process is very fast with exothermic).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0007_R_d173573638e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The uptake of inactive Na1 is more difficult than its release during the reciprocal migration of Na1↔Na2. Furthermore, most migration barriers of Na2→Na1 pathways in MLNP are below those of NVP (0.45&nbsp;eV), promoting the uptake of inactive Na1. This trend effectively excites the Na1 site and contributes to the higher capacity compared with NVP. Moreover, the paths in MLNP (path 1 and 2) increase the migration difficulty, in the light of their exceeded barriers with NVP. Around the diverse TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra in MLNP, the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> needs to pass through the bottleneck of different degrees, while in NVP, the migration mode in same sites needs to overcome the same migration energy barrier due to the only VO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. In specific, the path 1 and 2 are likely to related to the alteration of local environments with specific redox reactions. Benefitting from the energy gain, the migration of Na2 →Na1 makes inactive Na1 exist in Na2 ↔ Na2 migration process. The barrier for such a process is faster in MLNP (≈0.55–0.61&nbsp;eV) than that of pure NVP (0.70&nbsp;eV) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0003">3b,c</a>. Therefore, MLNP, the introduction of multilevel redox reaction in NVP, brings the barrier down in the various Na<sup>+</sup> migration patterns. The low diffusion energy barrier boosts the development for the outstanding rate performance of MLNP in SIBs. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0003">3d</a> records the rate performance of MLNP cells from 0.1to 20 C. All the <i>C</i><sub>s</sub> of MLNP half cells are above 95&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> up to 5 C, whose GCD curves maintain characteristic potential plateaus in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0003">3e</a>. Furthermore, the average <i>C</i><sub>s</sub> remains 59.01 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C, which obviously exceeds the counterparts of NVP (51.28 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information. And the two kinds of half cells recover to reversible capacity manifesting the initial level after the high currents via stable de-/intercalation process of Na<sup>+</sup>. The overall performance of MLNP is higher than that of NVP. Based on these electrochemical properties and the calculation result, a remarkable difference between MLNP and NVP in lacking V<sup>4+/5+</sup> and unsustainable cycle performance are likely to be associated with the diffusion of adjacent Na<sup>+</sup> and DOS distribution around the TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedron from the unpaired 3d orbital electrons in TMs. Moreover, the local structures diversify effectively breaks the high barrier of phase transition, probably decomposed by progressive redox reactions, further accommodating sodium storage and gaining a respectable performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0025_R_d173573638e1083" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Eng. J.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202400690-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f802feae-20c4-4a84-ada2-8986e71d5518/adma202400690-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f802feae-20c4-4a84-ada2-8986e71d5518/adma202400690-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/44fedf4f-cc4f-4a1c-a4b3-17db68679cc0/adma202400690-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f802feae-20c4-4a84-ada2-8986e71d5518/adma202400690-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202400690-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202400690" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The comparison of migration energy barriers within the different Na<sup>+</sup> groups of MLNP and NVP in pathways of Na1↔Na2. b) The comparison of migration energy barriers within the different Na<sup>+</sup> groups of MLNP and NVP in pathways of Na2↔Na2. c) Calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion pathways of Na2↔Na2 and the corresponding migration energy barriers. d) Rate capability at varied current densities. e) The corresponding GCD curves of rate testing.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202400690-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202400690-sec-0060-title">2.4 Structural Evolution and Na Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>The stepwise redox reactions in the MLNP cells were revealed by in situ and ex situ techniques systematically in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0004">4</a>. In situ XRD technique was employed to investigate the overall evolution of the crystal structure of MLNP, aiming to investigate the reason for the balance between its structural stability and powerful ion diffusion ability. In particular, the specific diffraction peaks with strong reflections, including (006), (024), (211), (116), and (300), are monitored for insertion/extraction behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> during the first two cycles. The position of the diffraction peak shifts toward higher angles compared with NVP, indicating a certain shrinkage of the crystal structure. In the electrochemical process, these peak reflections of MLNP gradually shift rightward and turn back reversibly, indicating the moderate solid-solution process, without phase transformation or boundaries produced. The disappearance/emergence of the reflection peaks in NVP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0004">4b</a>) is significantly different from MLNP in the same testing pattern. The phase transformation reactions usually involve additional kinetic barriers for initiation of a new phase and its boundary propagation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0012_R_d173573638e1132" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> so are the phase transformation reactions in NVP, in coincidence with the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barriers mentioned in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0003">3</a>. While the peaks’ migration reveals the high reversibility (both peak intensity and angle) of the two upon cycling, verifying the reversible reaction process and further confirming their robust structure. And the movement back and forth also implies the degree of structural change: the shift of NVP spans greater than MLNP, thus causing large deformation during the same charge/discharge process. Therefore, there is an obvious gap between MLNP and NVP in structural evolution mechanism.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202400690-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/68518a12-e4c9-4c6d-9e7f-4075b11d6ac1/adma202400690-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/68518a12-e4c9-4c6d-9e7f-4075b11d6ac1/adma202400690-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ce01d8f5-c5fb-4249-9176-b86b930cfb0f/adma202400690-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/68518a12-e4c9-4c6d-9e7f-4075b11d6ac1/adma202400690-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202400690-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202400690" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ XRD contour map during the initial two cycles at 0.1 C of a) MLNP cathode; b) NVP cathode. c) Ex situ XPS of high-resolution Fe 2p, Cr 2p, V 2p and Ti 2p spectra at different charge/discharge states.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Further, ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was applied to investigate the feasible Na storage mechanism of MLNP in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0004">4c</a>. The valence state is evaluated by the peak-fitting results of the fine spectrum, the specific components of TMs are confirmed in the different states, implying the successional oxidization (①–④) of Ti<sup>3+/4+</sup>, Fe<sup>2+/3+</sup>, V<sup>3+/4+</sup>, V<sup>4+/5+</sup>, and Cr<sup>3+/4+</sup> in the charge process and progressive reduction (⑤–⑨) of Cr<sup>4+/3+</sup> and V<sup>5+/4+</sup>, V<sup>4+/3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+/2+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+/3+</sup>, V<sup>3+/2+</sup> in the discharge process, labeled by the serial number in the first cycle and distinguished by the potential of redox reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0024_R_d173573638e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Energy Lett.">24</a>, <a href="#adma202400690-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0026_R_d173573638e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Phys. Chem. C Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fine XPS spectrum of Fe and Cr restores to the initial state after discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V, while counterparts of Ti and V retain a partial low valence state and contribute reversible capacity in subsequent cycles offsetting the irreversible loss of performance. Not only multivalence state of the V element goes from +2 to +5 during the electrochemical course, but also progression of each TM redox couple drives adequate Na<sup>+</sup> storage, revealing the multielectron-transfer ability in MLNP.</p>
            
            <p>The multiple electron reaction of MLNP reduces the migration barrier and obtains good diffusion ability of Na<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, the multilevel redox makes the structural evolution of MLNP proceed in a homeostatic solid solution manner, which is different from the phase transition reaction of NVP. The change of lattice parameters with the state of charge (SOC) was calculated by the Bragg formula during the first two cycles and shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0005">5a</a>. The cell parameter c drops at the final state of discharge progress in both two cycles (indicated by dashed circle), which is against the expansion with continuous Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, more possibly proceeding internal structure reform to buffer the unusual change. Such a change is a common phenomenon during the electrochemical process of low valence (Fe<sup>2+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>) substitution in NVP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0010_R_d173573638e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett. J. Mater. Sci. Technol. Adv. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202400690-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0011_R_d173573638e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Sci. Chem. Eng. J.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202400690-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0013_R_d173573638e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202400690-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0027_R_d173573638e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Combined with the results of characterization and the migration barrier, the electron filling and magnetic transformation have manifested the evident twists within the crystal structure of MLNP compared to NVP. In addition, Na<sup>+</sup> migration around Fe-V (paths 1 and 2) needs to overcome a higher potential barrier than the original NVP. Therefore, it is speculated that the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> around the FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron may be the key factor to drive steady-state solid solution reaction in MLNP. And the comparison of volume change (∆<i>V</i>) is listed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0005">5b</a> among the common NASICON materials, distinguishing the amount of electrochemically available Na<sup>+</sup> by different colors. MLNP possesses a quite low ∆<i>V</i> (just 1.74%), far blowing other NASICON materials de-/intercalating less than 2 Na<sup>+</sup>, let alone more than 2 Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0028_R_d173573638e1243" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Nano Electrochim. Acta J. Am. Chem. Soc. Small Methods Chem. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the exceptional cycling durability of MLNP is attributed to its minimal cell variation and structural evolution.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202400690-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/872e9046-5ca9-4396-ae3e-a4cc4cee1755/adma202400690-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/872e9046-5ca9-4396-ae3e-a4cc4cee1755/adma202400690-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ed4a6861-3f95-4579-be30-09be89cf67b5/adma202400690-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/872e9046-5ca9-4396-ae3e-a4cc4cee1755/adma202400690-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202400690-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202400690" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The unit cell volume variation along with SOC during the electrochemical course in MLNP. b) Comparison of volume variation of MLNP with other typical NASICON-structured cathodes for SIBs. c) The PDOS for different occupied configurations of Na<sup>+</sup> in MLNP; d) the PDOS at the fully occupied state of Na<sup>+</sup> in NVP. e) ICOHP comparison of MLNP and NVP with different occupied states of Na<sup>+</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Moreover, the electronic structures created from the coordination environments of different TM ions were examined for the influence on structural evolution with the cell variation in MLNP. Investigated by the DFT, the direct connection of O atom to Na<sup>+</sup> in the migration channels makes ambient TM atoms influence the electronic structure of O atom in both MLNP and NVP. Thus, the PDOS of O atom is used to directly reflect the pivotal characteristic of MLNP and NVP. The PDOS of MLNP with varying Na<sup>+</sup> occupied states are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0005">5c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information. The O-2p state distribution with many peaks near the Fermi level, which is more obvious in the state of MLNP without Na<sup>+</sup> fully occupied. These peaks are contributed by the interaction of O atom and different TM atoms, which evenly distributed (or covered) the band gap near the Fermi level. Thus, in the MLNP, the peaks will form a “state-ladder” for easily transferring electron from valence band to conduction band. In addition, for MLNP with full Na<sup>+</sup> occupied, these peaks will be fusion and overlap to form a smooth state for electron transfer. Overall, the multiple TM─O bonds will lead the O-2p orbital forming a “state-ladder” access and lowering the band gap to accelerate&nbsp;the electron transferring in MLNP. However, NVP still has an obvious band gap even with a full Na<sup>+</sup> occupied state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0005">5d</a>), identifying the indispensability of multiple TM─O bonds. Furthermore, the bond strength of TM─O is also investigated by integrated crystal orbital Hamiltonian population (ICOHP), and the corresponding results are collected in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0005">5e</a>. It is seen that the V─O and Ti─O in MLNP provide the stronger bond strength compared with the counterpart (V─O) in NVP, whatever with the different occupied states of Na<sup>+</sup>. With the removal of Na<sup>+</sup>, the bonding actions of TM─O are enhanced in most cases, and the bonding actions divert the strain variation by diversified TM─O in MLNP. Especially, the interaction of V─O bond with a more negative ICOHP value in MLNP is stronger than that of progressively enhanced Ti─O since the Na2 fully removing, differing from the occupied states (the full and Na1/Na2 = 1). Therefore, the same bonds of V─O in MLNP display obvious enhancement than that of NVP, further identifying its superior structural stability. The exception in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0005">5e</a> reveals diminished bond strength of Fe─O during the further removal on Na2 site during the ending of charge progress, which is likely related to the abnormal change of cell parameters in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0005">5a</a>. Accordingly, the robust frame of MLNP guarantees the structural stability, by virtue of the Ti─O and V─O participation. In brief, the multistep redox boosts the development of electrochemical behavior to convert a homeostatic solid solution course innovatively. MLNP takes full advantage of multielectron chemistry with low strain for achieving remarkable performance in the charge/discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0029_R_d173573638e1312" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Dalton Trans.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202400690-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202400690-sec-0070-title">2.5 Practical Application and Multilevel Redox Superiority</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the practical application of MLNP, the sodium ion full cells (SIFCs) were assembled by MLNP and hard carbon (HC) as the cathode and anode, respectively, denoted as HC||MLNP. And the sodium storage behavior of SIFCs is shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0006">6a</a>, whose HC electrode is after the pre-sodiation process to reduce its irreversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0030_R_d173573638e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Eng. J.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding characterization and electrochemical properties of HC are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0006">6b</a>, the corresponding initial GCD curve at 0.1 C displays 143.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the range of ≈1.2–4.4&nbsp;V, calculated by the mass of cathode active materials. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0006">6c</a> displays the rate performance of the SICFs from 0.1 to 5 C: the stable <i>C</i><sub>s</sub> of SIFCs outputs at different rates and still maintains 70 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 5 C, indicating the great high-rate capability. And the capacity retention of SIFCs at 0.5 C is achieved 77% after 200 cycles even with the unsatisfactory cycling stability of HC anode, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0006">6d</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202400690-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202400690-bib-0031_R_d173573638e1358" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the multilevel redox of MLNP retains decent properties in SIFCs. Moreover, the preponderant <i>C</i><sub>s</sub> and energy density of MLNP are highlighted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0006">6e</a> over those of the state-of-the-art typical NASICON compounds, due to the gradual activation of redox couples with the transfer of 2.77 e<sup>−</sup>.The homeostatic solid solution course with multistep redox enables MLNP favorable ion/electron migration kinetics greatly to facilitate the sodium storage. Its impressive energy density of ≈440&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the active mass of cathode) demonstrates the great practical applicability of the MLNP cathode. More particularly, the durable capacity retention of MLNP in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202400690-fig-0006">6f</a> outperforms the advanced NASICON cathodes during long-term cycling. It delivers low to 0.0061% capacity decay per cycle at a high rate (5 C). The detailed data can be seen in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information. The remarkable lead reveals the progressive effect of multiple redox couples in MLNP thus suppressing the continuous strain accumulation. Therefore, the progressive redox reaction of MLNP promotes the rapid migration of Na<sup>+</sup>, establishing the balance of multi-electron transfer and low strain.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202400690-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cb47442c-086c-4e24-a319-7c3f3361981b/adma202400690-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cb47442c-086c-4e24-a319-7c3f3361981b/adma202400690-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/88e4f7a4-9aa4-42ca-89ad-de708a5f32a7/adma202400690-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cb47442c-086c-4e24-a319-7c3f3361981b/adma202400690-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202400690-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202400690" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of HC||MLNP SIFCs. b) GCD curve of HC||MLNP SIFCs. c) Rate performance at different current densities; d) cycling performance of SIFCs. Comparison of e) <i>C</i><sub>s</sub> and energy density and f) capacity retention during long cycling with other typical NASICON cathodes in SIBs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202400690-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202400690-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, MLNP shows homeostatic solid solution characteristics and higher structural stability compared with but not limited to NVP. The fascinating electrochemical performance of MLNP meets the pursuit of energy density (440&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>), utilizing the wide valence state of V element (+2→+5) in its low strain strong frame structure by the differentiation of each redox couple, establishing the balance of multi-electron transfer and low strain. Consequently, a superior cycling durability with 0.0061% capacity decay per cycle is achieved after 5000 cycles at 5 C. The fast and stable reaction kinetics stems from the release/uptake of inactive Na1 and depressed energy barrier confirmed by DFT computations. This work delicately incorporates multiple redox couples, decomposing the high barrier of intrinsic mono-redox center with sluggish reactivity, by modulating the reaction route to develop high-performance NASICON cathodes for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202400690-sec-0090-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
